Hey everybody. In all living things, there is a supporting system of prokaryotes and other microbes that are feeding off of the carbon that these systems provide. And they're also providing for the maintenance and the health of these systems by providing all the little things that they need that they get in exchange for the carbon. And one of the things that Albert Howard talks about is the law of returns, where the things that are produced on the top of the soil need to go back to feed what was taken out from the bottom of the soil from the decay cycle. So whenever a plant is growing and taking all this wonderful carbon and putting it in the soil, the soil is actually supporting the plant in such a way that it's depleting its natural resources with the expectation that when this plant dies, all of that stored carbon will get returned. And so it's willing to go negative in the producing of the plant, knowing that later on it's going to get this wonderful harvest of the actual plant itself when it dies. And so there's this tremendous aggregate accumulation of organic matter through this process because the tree, of course, is where the plant is bringing in all this carbon, and then most of it is gonna get accumulated under the soil when the plant dies. A lot of it's gonna get taken off and uh into the atmosphere, but uh much of it will be biologically processed by the soil and return into organic matter in the soil, which will later be fed into another plant. And this whole thing will accumulate more and more as because there's no place for the carbon to go, what little is escaping is fine, that's okay because most of it is getting returned to the soil, which is accumulating. Now, what we do when we harvest, when we harvest a plant, we take that plant out. And just to show you, I would love to leave my ochre plants on the uh my soil to decay and rot, and but I don't have time for that. So over here you can see this is my okra plant. That's how much carbon I took out of my garden. I harvested the whole thing. I left the roots in, of course, because they aren't RNA thing, but I had to take the bulk of it out because there's just too much mass. I'm trying to grow other things in that space. So all that carbon came out. I have to replace that with well-made humus. If I had a more regenerative cycle, if I had more time, if I had more land, I would go through crop cycles where I would leave the crop to decay on the land and that would create a carbon return. I could use animals for this process as well. But because I'm doing an active rotational garden, I have to take the plant off. I need that space. So that's why I have to add a bunch of humus because my organic matter is too depleting the way I garden. Now when I add the organic matter, I reverse that whole process because I can physically add tons and tons of organic matter right onto the soil that the microbes will feed off and use to produce more plants. And this is a wonderful way of doing it because you are manually entering the organic matter. Anyway, I just want to give a little video about Howard, Albert Howard's law of returns, that what you take out you need to put in so that you're not depleting the soil and creating what he would call soil banditry. You need to be good to your soil and put in more than you take out. Anyway, thanks, y'all.

And so all these three things are gonna be the heavy nitrogen stuff, and the wood chips are gonna be the heavy carbon stuff. Mix them together, you get that wonderful hot compost for the nitrogen and the carbon bond together, make the best soil known to man. Anyway, let's get started. The first thing we do is create a foundation of wood chips. This is what all of the stinky nitrogen stuff is going to sit on top of. And I have some wood chips and some brush and things like that. And the idea is to completely capture the nitrogen so it does not smell, and so that all of your compost is full of nitrogen because that's the problem. Nitrogen wants to escape. It's a very light gas. Everything is trying to keep the nitrogen in place so that you plants can use it. And so this bonding process is really fundamental because there's not a really good way, other than nitrogen chemical pellets made from a giant factory somewhere to get the nitrogen in there if your soil is hard. Now, if you have soft soil, then atmospheric pressure can do it, and legumes can also do it, and there's lots of good ways. But starting soil off with lots of nitrogen is really the way to go. And so I'm covering all this car or this nitrogen stuff up with heavy carbon, uh, which are the wood chips, and this will lock the nitrogen in. As the nitrogen tries to escape through evaporation, gets such a light gas, it will run into the carbon that's on top. It will bond with the carbon. Uh, the microbes in there are grabbing that nitrogen just as quickly as it moves and bonding it with the carbon. That's what they do. So the idea is to have a really uh is to completely cover all that vegetable refuse and manure with carbon. And here's what you have after. It's a really nice pile, no scent, everything is just beautiful. Hey everybody. I get asked uh this question a lot. How do I put up with the stench of my compost piles? Aren't my neighbors complaining? I mean, this has got to smell horrible. I have a bunch of rotting organic matter, a bunch of old vegetables rotting in here. What's going on? And the the truth is it doesn't smell at all. I'm standing right next to it, and I could be standing right next to it on top of it and digging into it the day after I made it and you wouldn't smell it. And that's because we are actively capturing all that nitrogen. That's the stuff that smells. That's the very active gas that has all the particles of the food with it, and that's what's escaping and making all this smell real bad. We're capturing all that with carbon. And the last thing we do when we're capturing all that, when we're making our mound or pile, if there's a lot of rotting vegetables on top there, we'll carbon cup it. We'll put a layer of leaves or wood chips over the top of that, and so any nitrogen escaping gets caught into this carbon web and sinks and returns. If your compost pile, if your hot compost pile smells, that means you're losing nitrogen. That means you need to add more carbon. Particularly need to add more carbon on top of your pile so that the escaping nitrogen gets caught in that web in that active filter. But if you're not doing that, then your pile is gonna stink and you're losing the nitrogen. That's your you're trying to capture that. That's the whole point of this, is so that your humus is nitrogen rich. Anyway, stinky piles are bad. Don't let that happen to you. If your humus, if your uh compost is stinky, add more carbon. Put wood chips on that. If there's exposed garbage that smells, put wood chips on top of that. Put leaves on top of that, put a carbon source on top of that, and that scent will go right away. Anyway, thanks, y'all.

Hey everybody. So why do I bother spending all my time and energy making compost when I can just buy fertilizer? Why not use fertilizers? And the reason I think it's important not to use fertilizers is that I think the carbon content of your soil, the amount of organic matter in your soil, will represent the health of the soil. The biological churning of the microbes and earthworms and crickets and all the other things in that soil is basically refreshing the organic matter in there, the life in there, and it's protecting us and keeping it current with our environment. I think it's important to feed off of living things when we're trying to rescue our health, because I think that's become a real problem with the autoimmune disorders and just essentially the breakdown of our immune systems. That the real reason to get that immune system going is to create carbon-rich soil. And what happens when you use fertilizers is that you go around the microbes that would normally be feeding the plants their micro or their nutrients and getting a little bit of carbon exchange, this great currency that's going for this great exchange that's going back and forth between the microbes and the plants gets disrupted by the fertilizer because you're certain you feed the plants directly now. You're giving the plants what they want in quantities they want, which is going to start putting pressure on the carbon supply of the soil because everything will start feeding the plant more of the carbon out of the soil because they know that they're going to get a great big harvest of carbon when that plant comes down and dies. All that carbon will be returned with extra carbon that's feeding out of the atmosphere. So in the meantime, they're willing to take a little bit of carbon in the form of an exudate from the plant while giving the plant more of the carbon reserves than it would like to normally, you know, that we do is using up the carbon reserves to grow the plant. No, knowing that it's going to come back trifle. It's it's an investment. But when you take the plant away, when you harvest it, the microbes don't get that return. And so putting that fertilizer on there and and bumping up all the other nutrients so the carbon exhaustion gets bumped up as well. Well, when you harvest, of course, then that carbon is depleted out. If you add more fertilizers as opposed to adding more carbon and more humus and more organic matter, you'll get this depletion further and further, and eventually you'll get to a point where there's not enough organic matter in the soil to have a real lively population of microbes and earthworms and crickets and all those other things that are going to be contributing to the plant's health. That biological churning that I think is important, I think it's missing in our food. So if you use fertilizers, I think that in the end you're going to have less biological churning because you're replacing the microbes that would normally be doing all that, but also that you're depleting the carbon out of the soil. Hi everybody. A key concept in understanding the decay cycle when looking at soil, when looking at your agricultural, uh looking at your gardening, is to understand digestion. Hey everybody, I think a key concept in understanding regenerative agriculture is to understand the concept of the decay cycle and the growth cycle. Everything in life is made up of carbon chains. These carbon chains are formed of carbon, of course, that link gases and salts, the gases, carbon, hydrogen, nitrogen, and a variety of different salts. And then an electric current fuses all this together. That electric current, of course, is life. And once that current ceases, all of those atoms are going to recombine and form carbon dioxide and nitrogen and things and go back off into the atmosphere, where they will have to be re-collected by plants, of course, which collect carbon out of the air and recirculated into the environment again. Now, this is the growth cycle where plants take carbon out of the atmosphere to make the building blocks the carbon chains of life. The decay cycle is where those carbon chains that have already been pulled and formed, and some of them have died, some of them have not, are reformed by other life into new carbon chains, refusing them and basically recycling the organic matter back into the environment. And so there's the growth cycle, which takes it out of the atmosphere and the growth and the decay cycle, which recycles it back into the environment, where it can be reused indefinitely, constantly getting its carbon broken apart and reformed into new carbon chains. And this is the cycle of life which allows for organic matter to get built up into huge amounts of topsoil, which promote huge plants, which promote all sorts of life because all life is at some point dependent upon the growth cycle, which is plants. Plants take the carbon out of the atmosphere as the cycle starts, but a healthy environment is going to have a healthy decay cycle where that environment, where that carbon is constantly getting recirculated and fed back to the plants and fed back to us. The problem with our current environment, our current agriculture is that we completely don't even acknowledge the decay cycle. We focus entirely on the growth cycle. So it's larger tomatoes, larger this, all the while we just eviscerate the soil. And the decay cycle, which should make, which should be accumulating organic matter within the soil to feed to the plants, is no longer even there. The biology that would do this whole job is gone. And that's part of the problem is that we don't acknowledge that the decay cycle is based upon a biology, a biology that's constantly digesting organic matter and purifying it by doing this and refeeding it and refeeding it back into the environment. It keeps it current so we can feed it to the plants so that we can then eat it from the plants or from the animals which feed the plants or which eat the plants. The whole thing is dependent upon a growth cycle and a decay cycle that are in balance. And the problem with modern agriculture and our modern food system is that they are not. And so a big concept with regenerative agriculture is to cultivate the decay cycle, to invigorate the soil full of microbes and life. And this is why I'm always adding humus in the form of broken down, well-made compost into my garden on the top of my soil, so that all the life, all the earthworms and all the different microbes and the prokaryotes can eat off this and then feed it back into the plants, and the plants will feed them the carbon that they're looking for. And this wonderful cycle that builds up the soil and promotes the plant's health. Anyway, uh, decay cycle, just wanted to get a little video about it. Thanks, y'all.

Hi everybody. I feel that regenerative agriculture can be summed up in Albert Howard's law of returns. So Albert Howard talked about how the organic matter that you take off the land through crops and uh through through the conventional high-calorie crops that our agriculture is. Because we're at the top of the food chain. So the calories we eat are the densest. They take the most out of the land. So if you just continue to do this, you deplete the land, and suddenly you're not growing those crops anymore. The land doesn't have the ability to produce those uh calories. And so what you have to do is replenish the land with organic matter, and that would in old times have been manure and plant residue like straws and stock and all the leftover stuff that's not the fruit, and you would put that with the animal manure, and this would create a rich compost, a rich human high humus compost that they would put back on the soils and this would replenish them. Albert Howard's law of returns would be simply that the amount of organic matter that you remove through these high-calorie plants and foods that you're stripping off the land, what ratio is that to what you're putting back on the land through manuring, through animal disturbance, through going fallow and things of that nature, the things that replenish the land. And so if you're doing those well, you should build organic matter and build fertility, and you will have a better field in the future. And if you're not, then you'll be depleting the field and you'll have a worse field that will grow fewer calories in the future. And eventually, if you're in the wrong climate, maybe even go desert, you're in the right calorie, just kind of peter out. And so there's so very little fertility in the soil that it can't hold healthy, vigorous plants that produce a lot of calories because top of the food chain means the maximum amount of churning, biological churning to produce the plant that we need. So once that depletes, we can no longer grow eggplants with big, fat, juicy eggplants on full of calories. Consequently, the law of returns is just that what you put back is more than you take out, and you find some way of doing that so that the outcome is more organic matter, greater fertility in the future. Real simple. Anyway, thanks, y'all.

Hey everybody, I want to talk about crumb structure. Crumb structure is what allows high quality, good living, biologically churning soil to hold more water and more air than dead, beat up old soil. And the reason is is that the microbes in the living soil are busy bonding. Well, see, when they break everything down, that the finest level they turn everything into like a weird paste. And everything gets clumped together with this paste. It becomes a weird glue. Like if the only thing you could make a house out of would be bricks, then this goo would be like the cement. And it bonds all the inorganic particles to the organic particles and makes these big clumps of things, and these big clumps of things have more space between them. Just like uh if you like have a big box and you fill it full of sand, it won't hold that much water. But if you filled it full of like baseballs or bowling balls or some really large size, it would hold lots of water and lots of air. There'd be lots of space between those bowling balls. And so that's the same way with crumb structure, is that it's going to hold more of everything that the soil needs. It needs not just the water, which will hold a lot more, and that's great, but needs the air. And so that's a great way to kind of augment all the nice things that will be happening in your soil if it's soft, if it has good crumb structure, because this crumb structure, this biological goo is weak. It's strong enough to break up water, it's weak enough for roots to go right through it. So it has to be a real kind of fine, nice, easy to break up quality, and that gives it like a soft texture. But again, crumb structure, okay. Other than just you know, being cool about soil and all, crumb structure is a great name for uh um hardcore band, either metal or punk, I'll give it to you. And just think about the longer you think about, the better it becomes. Crumb structure. Oh, and by the way, if you have a better hardcore band name than crumb structure, leave it in the comments so I can ridicule it.

Hi everybody, just want to talk real quickly about how the gut bugs talk to each other. This is so interesting. Your cells are defined by their DNA. They they sit in a nucleus and they're protected. That's what they are. Blood cells, bone cells, things like this. But your bacteria, in fact, all bacteria have no nucleases. They're much, much smaller than what's called the eukaryotic cells, which are everything you see around you. But these small little bacteria have no nucleases and they share DNA directly across the cell membrane in what's called lateral gene transfer. This is very important because while you have to reproduce new cells to change them up a little bit, or change them just a little bit in other ways, these bacteria are constantly changing up. And that means they're constantly renewing and reviving based on the conditions and the circumstances and environments. And this is what makes them so dynamic, and this is why it's so important to be constantly giving them new sources of fresh, cool bacteria.

Hey everybody, why hot composting? Why not cold composting? Why not just bit it at the back of the beds where there's nothing going on, a little muck area, and just not bother it at all. And I'll tell you why. Uh, one of the big reasons is nitrogen capture. So when you do an anaerobic breakdown, a breakdown without oxygen, like the cold compost that everyone talks about, you aren't capturing the live, good nitrogen that's in the waste, in the grass clippings, in the vegetable waste, and the in the animal animal manures and all these things are high in nitrogen, that's why they stink. And when you don't capture that, the humus that you make will be lacking nitrogen. Because nitrogen wants to evaporate, it's real light. It just zips right out. It's one of the very first things. It competes with water in terms of how fast it it it uh leaves organic matter, just real quick. And that's what organic matter needs. It needs that zip, it needs its nitrogen. So when you hot compost, you create an atmosphere, or you create an environment, sorry, where the compost is absorbing the nitrogen. You can tell because it doesn't stink. I put stinky vegetables in there, covered it, mixed it, carbon everywhere, carbon absorbs that nitrogen, and there's no stink from day one to when it finishes off in day 30 or 45, 60, or whatever it finishes off. But it ideally all that nitrogen is captured with the carbon bonds in the organic matter in all these carbon chains that are newly formed from the old carbon chains have been broken down. This is crucial because everything needs nitrogen, it's one of the very first things that's exhausted in your garden. So having organic ma or humus that's rich in nitrogen is great for your garden. You'll see it. Now, do you need to use that pretty quickly? Because it will the nitrogen want to escape from that, even though it's kind of bonded? Yes. Absolutely. You need to make it cure it get it on the bed get plants going into it and that will preserve that nitrogen utilizing it for the plants. And so the first thing you want to do is hurry up and use that because nitrogen goes quick. Anyway that's one of the big things about gardening is you realize how quickly nitrogen depletion happens. And so a good way to just kind of prevent that is have large amounts of humus that's rich in nitrogen. You get that through a good hot compost because you're capturing that nitrogen. Cold compost just kind of sifts out also they stink. Anyway just want to make a video about nitrogen capture because it's so gosh dang important. Thanks y'all

Now, nature in all their its systems is constantly dropping organic matter in the form of leaves and bug carcasses and old trees and grasses and everything else onto the ground, and that gets decayed into the uh soil and digested into the soil through the decay cycle. And this is nature's method of creating more organic matter into the ground, which creates greater fertility, which can grow larger plants, and then the production value is based on a balance within the decay cycle and the growth cycle. In conventional agriculture, we abandon the decay cycle, focusing entirely on the growth cycle, and eventually the soil is depleted of its organic matter, and everything is based upon chemical additives. And what we could be doing is taking the organic material out of it, cleaning it through the composting, hot composting method, and adding it to the fields to increase fertility. This increased fertility, this biology that you're adding to the field would help with everything. Hey everybody. Every so often I'll hear somebody, and by every so often I mean all the time, I hear people talk about giving their plants nutrients. And you gotta give your plants nutrients because they they feed off nutrients that helps them create the building blocks of of their structure and nutrients and nutrients and blah blah blah. I don't give my plants nutrients, uh, and I really don't believe in the concept. I think that there's a miss here where people think that biological entities, complex ones like ourselves and the plants and things like this, all absorb nutrients because they're directly given these nutrients, and they can, but this isn't a very good system. The plants and the nutrients have a gap between them. This gap is filled by the microbes. But microbes are actually what break down the polymers into monomers, nutrients, and then provide them to the plants exactly as the plants need them, exactly as the plants ask for them. There's communication between the plants and the microbes. The microbes deliver exactly what the plants need, and in return the plants return carbon to the soil, and that's what the microbes want. So there's this exchange going on, but the plants don't absorb nutrients directly, they're fed these nutrients by the microbes. And when we go around that process, when we provide the ammonia, for instance, directly to the plants to absorb, they can, but without any sort of regulation, without any sort of interaction, without any sort of exchange. This isn't healthy for the plants. This isn't the ideal way for them to go about their business of incorporating nutrients. And so I don't worry about nutrients because I know that giving the plants nutrients directly is not a good idea. I want to provide organic matter that microbes can break down and then feed the plants from that. There's a process of biological churning within the soil that's really what you're looking for. Not nutrients. You're looking for microbes. You're looking for a good biological churn where there's a lot of organic matter for those microbes to work from. And that's the way nature does it. It's a much better system than us providing nutrients. So don't think about nutrients. Think about microbes. Think about providing microbes with raw material to break down and give to your plants, but don't give them the raw material itself. They don't want it. They don't need it. It's not exactly how it's done. And it leads to all sorts of problems with the plant and its resilience to disease and pestilence. Because those nutrients that the that you're giving directly are not form-fitted the way the microbes are going to form fit those nutrients. Where they're going to get exactly what they need, exactly how they need it, exactly when they need it. And you can't do that by just giving them raw nutrients. So don't do it. Give them biology, give them microbes, give them organic matter, and watch your garden just flourish without any sort of over or under or any sort of deficiencies or any sort of uh pestilence problems or disease problems. Because when the microbes feed the plants, you get a health that you can't really define through normal conventional means. It explodes with health. It gives you health in ways that you wouldn't even imagine. Anyway, don't worry about your nutrients. Worry about your microbes. Or at least that's what I do. Anyway, thanks, y'all.

They just come and drop it off because they have to pay to dump it, and we'll take them for free. And then we add the kitchen waste, and it's about two parts uh wood chips, one part kitchen waste, and then you add a bunch of water, mix it up as well as you can, and you let it sit. And it should heat up to about 130 degrees, and then the breakdown of that organic material will be very thorough. You'll find the wood chips become very small, and the food waste becomes gone, and it will heat up very nicely for about two months, and in that time you'll have to toss it or uh take the big tractor that we have back there, you can see it, and turn it over, give it a little bit more water if it's not raining enough. And you do this two or three times over a two-month period, and that aerates it and mixes it at the same time. That's what you're looking to do, and that will reheat the whole pile as it starts to cool down, then it'll use up all the oxygen and cool down, then you mix it again, and the whole thing starts over again. Hi everybody. Everything you can perceive that's life is a eukaryot. And that's the plants, the animals, the insects, the fungus, and all those weird in-between creatures at the bottom of the ocean floor. They're basically cells that have a membrane as well as a nucleus inside the membrane full of DNA. This DNA is what defines the function of the cell. So in any particular eukaryot cell, you can cut open the nucleus, look at its DNA, and say, oh, that's the uh toenail of an elephant, or oh, that's the root of an azalea, or something of that nature. You can identify what it is by its DNA. That's it's uh that's how we define it. Now there's a whole nother world that's much older that this eukaryotic world sits on top of, and that's the prokaryotic world. Their cells are made up very differently. Their cells are just a single membrane, no nucleus, and the DNA sits right along the edge of this membrane and goes back and forth into the environment and the cell. It literally takes its DNA and makes it something transient, going back and forth. So when it needs the DNA, or when the DNA better fits the environment, takes it on, and when it no longer fits, because it can only take on so much DNA at once, it will let that DNA go and take on the better fitting DNA. And so it's constantly fitting its environment to itself through the DNA. This is called lateral gene transfer, and it makes the bacteria and the archaea the two components of this world because they have different membranes. Hi everybody. I want to talk about tillage and how I think it fits into regenerative agriculture. Uh uh there's a lot of no-till people and theories and uh that's just a word, you know, that's very commonly used, and it can be uh it can be a lot of different things, and it's certainly uh part of regenerative agriculture that you are trying to disturb the soil as little as possible. And so I want to talk about the two different types of tillage and basically Parrot and Albert Howard point that not all tillage is the same, and that the common tillage that we use now to incorporate fertilizers and to make sure that the weeds are suppressed is what's called mold board uh plow tillage, where you take the soil and you run a blade underneath it that's at an angle and you scoop up the soil and you invert it. And you take the top six inches, eight inches, foot, whatever it is, and you basically take what was on top and bury it under six inches, and what was under that six inches at the bottom, putting it on top. And this destroys the seeds that we're setting up, and it puts the the fertilizer that you want in the soil, does all these good things, and uh they also say it aerates the soil because you put air into the into the soil as you turn it. But this is actually one of the most destructive things in terms of creating a positive decay cycle within the soil, because in the soil naturally set up is a process where the decay cycle at the surface is very extreme. It has all the weathering events like rain and wind and sun and heavy active breakdown from all these heavy elements. And then as it gets buried by other organic matter falling on top of it, that breakdown, which has already begun, will start to occur at a more biological level, and the little small organisms will start to eat it and digest it and make it smaller and and turn it into something that will begin to decay better with less sunlight, with less wind, with just a moisture and not an active pounding rain, and the slower decay cycles will become more effective. And the subterranean areas where the earthworms start to work and where they appreciate the dark and where they're creating natural aeration. That's how you do it. And so when you invert it, you turn all this topsy turvy and you take the things that want it dark and cool and without a lot of wind, and you put them to the extreme exposure, and you take all the things that need the extreme exposure and you put them all the slow decay areas. And so you do this several times and you basically destroy all the little microbes that are doing their decay cycle dance. You basically invert everything so that the decay cycle can't catch up. And a lot of people will consider it continually do this until the weeds are knocked out, and that's a real good son that the biology has suffered so tremendously that it's really going to be hard for those weeds to take hold for living aspects to take hold, and you're just going to grow your one crop irrigated and fertilized so that everything else is kind of suffered from it. And whenever you see tillage, regardless of which fertilizer they use, you almost always see the same thing, dead soil. And this is just the reality of the way we do our agriculture now. But there is a other type of till, another type of tillage that would be called like subsoiling, where you just drag a rod through hard compacted uh soil to break it up. And you're not really digging or diverting it, you're just basically taking some rods uh maybe a foot apart or something like that, and dragging them across the field and creating a minor disturbance, and this shatters the pan, that hard pan that the the water is hitting and running off. So when you're applying compost or something like that, you can just break the pan, incorporate it. And this is actually something that doesn't disturb the decay cycle that much and helps you with the adding the new amendments into something, shattering the pan before you try to add the amendments, and you apparently it gets more effective this way. And this tillage can and done the right way when you're adding compost and amendments, it can be good. That can be beneficial. You're it can help the decay cycle and help the incorporation of that new organic matter. And so in that way, that tillage is good. So again, the whole point is that tillage isn't necessarily good or bad. It depends on the disturbance level that you're doing and how you're doing it, why you're doing it. And if you're improving the decay cycle or if you're hindering the decay cycle, and to think about that when you're disturbing the soil. Anyway, thanks, y'all.

Hey everybody. I throw around the terms living food and dead food all the time, and no one knows what I mean. So I'm going to try to explain that. Living food is food that's grown in soil with a lot of microbial action, where compost, well-made compost is added to the soil, increasing the organic matter of the soil, the living part of the soil, so that the microbes and the prokaryotes, all the little bugs in the soil can start breaking apart that organic matter, reforming it into new organic matter, and then feeding it to the plants. And this new organic matter is fitted to its environment. And so what you get is a very specific nutrient value that's given to the plants, and this imparts a lot of natural protection to the plant. That it the microbes in the soil will hold off the decay cycle through their interaction with the plant. And when you replace these microbes with fertilizer, you get a lot less of the biological churning. You basically start feeding the plant directly as opposed to letting the microbes do it. And what this is going to create is sort of a plant that's separated from the natural biomes of the world that doesn't have this good protection against disease and pestilence. And so once you start fertilizing, you may increase your production initially, but eventually what you're going to find is that the plant loses its ability to bend off the natural pestilence and disease of the world, and you create a weak plant. And you eat enough of this food over a long enough period of time, in our case, maybe three generations, then eventually you start having your biome separated from the natural world and all its natural protections. And pretty soon you end up in a place that we are kind of at where our immune systems are slowly becoming unfunctional, and disease is is attacking us all over the place and new diseases that we've never even heard of because our biological protection has been removed. That we started feeding the plants with fertilizers, and now we're in a place where our plants no longer make us healthy. The food we eat and the animals that we consume from these plants are all sickly. And now we're venturing into a world where we have to attack disease constantly because the natural fortifications that we had are gone. We have to reintroduce living foods back into our diet, and this is the solution, the grand solution to our health problems. You know, uh there's a lot of little solutions along the way, but the big solution is we need to improve our gut biome. And the only way we can do that is associating it with living foods. The foods that come from living soils, we need to reconnect our bacteria to the world's bacteria. And so that's what I'm trying to do. I'm trying to get this message out that this food is fundamentally different from the supermarket. Anyway, thanks, y'all.

Yet another video why I prefer beneficial bacteria over probiotic bacteria. Beneficial bacteria is living in the median that or the medium that you are ingesting it in. The cabbage, the beets, the yogurt. That is where it's actually living. That's where it's stored. That's where it has all the things that it needs for its life, for its sustenance. Probiotic bacteria has been transferred from something like a peter dish where it was grown in its most ideal conditions, to something where it's not quite as ideal, as in a pill or some yogurt or a drink or some other food that was not its initial home, that wasn't there to cultivate its dynamic uh conditions that need that it needed to grow in the way it is. So whenever you change this over, you're you're even if you do keep it alive, this is something that's going to be very difficult for it to thrive in because these are not the ideal conditions for it. The ideal conditions were used in the petri dish where it was grown. Anyway, part seven coming up, y'all. Thanks.

Hey everybody. An interesting point that Albert Howard made in his works was that colonialism at least partially occurred because of the infertility of European soils to support their population. European farming was mostly done from a slash and burn agricultural standpoint where forests were cut down, slashed and burnt, and then the fertile ground underneath had been created from this forest constantly feeding organic material into the ground was used for production crops until the land was no longer sufficient for that, and then they would move on and cut down another chunk. And this is a great way to support a population, but it quickly used up the forests of Europe, and what happened was that there was too many people for too poor soil. And that big part of colonialism was to set up plantations all over the world and mine native soils for the fertility to support European crops. This is an interesting way of looking at it because eventually these soils also become uh infertile or less fertile because you mined out a lot of the organic matter, which happens when you're doing this uh production that doesn't have any sort of recontribution to the organic matter in the soil. So you just basically use it up. And what happens is that over a period of time you you find that the reaction to the soil is to compact and that locks out nitrogen. The nitrogen from the atmosphere no longer goes in it, the legumes no longer replenish it, and you start seeing nitrogen deficiencies everywhere. And so there is this great uh search for mostly bird dung to get its nitrogen value until the nitrogen pellet came about in the 50s, where or a little bit before that, where they finally chemically put nitrogen into a uh pellet fertilizer. Uh first they used it for bombs, but then they used it for fertilizers. But the the gist of this is that we have worked a system from time and world that's that's not functioning correctly. We need to have a system that contributes to the organic matter in the soil and not just depletes it to add chemicals. Chemical fertilizers have locked out the biology in the soil. And if you go to any cultivated areas that where it's been plowed and fertilized and pesticides have been used and even when they haven't it's been an organic farm, you'll try to find the the living element the soil and you're sift through it and you'll just find particulate matter there's almost nothing in it. And I I've looked around a lot of these soils and it's it's very interesting to see no earthworms, no beetles, nothing crawling and beyond that nothing too much living outside of you know even in at the microscopic level. Anyway, I want to give this quick video uh talking about that because it's a real interesting concept that a lot of our history has been shaped by the lack of fertility in our soils and the need for more fertility uh with and how this has shaped our histories. Anyway thanks

One of the things that I think is very positive and very encouraging when you look at the history of agriculture is the contribution of Chinese agriculture. I learned about through F. H. King, Farmers of Forty Centuries, but it was also brought up in some of the Albert Howard I like so much. And it's essentially the idea that China, in heavy population density, without changing lands, continuously farmed soil in such a way that they didn't lose fertility over 4,000 years. This was something that the Europeans and many of the uh other parts of the world uh could not manage to do. And so some of these early authors of the regenerative period went to China to go figure out what's going on. Like F. H. King's book was based on a tour deal, I think that was 1905, just to give you timeline. And what he found was that they had a system of canals that connected the population centers to the farm areas. And they would put all of the debris of society, but the countryside and everything else in these canals, and with a series of manual foot pumps, send this stuff down to the farmlands with water into a muck, but it was composting, so it wouldn't have been smelly or anything like that. There would have been a lot of carbon matter in this muck going down. And then at the end of the season or just whenever I guess uh they would the water would recede, they would stop the pumps, the debris that was down into the field areas they would drag onto the fields and they would compost. And this heavy nitrogen composting uh debris matter was fantastic fertilizer. And what they could do with you know the law of returns is the what they were taking off the crops or off the fields, they could just add back to it so they could do continuous crops that were heavy depletion without having to let the field go fallow or do any other methods that take time to replenish the carbon in the field. They could just use this canal method that was utilizing the waste from the cities, the waste, sending it down to the farmlands where it became processed into fertility. And if you think about that for a second, and our modern technology and how easy it would be to divert our waste out of this centralized process where we process it into big, heavily dense, toxic logs, but just to actually disperse it into farmlands where it is properly broken down, but where it is turned into fertility, where it's turned into organic matter so that you don't need to use fertilizers and pesticides, and you have to use less water where uh you can improve the soil and get the biological churning that we need so bad in our soils to start going. Anyway, thanks, y'all.

First question everybody asks about fermenting is what is the difference between fermenting and pickling? And they are fundamentally different, even though they seem like they have very similar results and they're both sour vegetables. The main difference is that vinegar is used in pickling and salt water is used in fermenting, and that's a fundamental difference because vinegar locks out bacteria of every type. Probiotic, beneficial, pathogenic, all the bacteria gets locked out. So anaerobic and aerobic bacteria both are removed in the pickling process. And the fermenting process is use salt water, which encourages beneficial anaerobic growth of bacteria. And what happens in turn in that is it locks out pathogenic bacteria that causes vegetables to rot and mold. Both of them are preservation methods. Both of them work fine for preserving preserving vegetables. But the big difference with fermenting is that it's so much healthier for you. Whenever you get this beneficial bacteria cultivated in the vegetable, it's like a pre-digestion for your food. It basically breaks down a lot of the food molecules the same way that your digestive tract will, because you digest anaerobically as well, which is uh without oxygen or it thrives in an environment without oxygen. And so basically, whenever you cultivate vegetables with this type of bacteria, you promote easy digestion, but you also teach your bacteria how to do a better job digesting your sugars and sarches. And that's because the probiotic bacteria found in fermented foods teaches your bacteria how to do a better job. It's a fascinating process, and very few things that actually help digestion do go that far to teach your bacteria how to do a better job. Anyway, I'll get back into this in my other videos, and uh I hope you have a great day. Thanks.

I wanted to make a quick video today about wild fermentation versus cultured fermentation. I am mostly up to this point just done wild fermentation, which is a cultivation of existing bacteria on the vegetable. So you take a raw vegetable, you uh submerge it in salt water, cutting off the oxygen, and that allows the anaerobic bacteria to flourish without any sort of competition, but you don't add any source order or culture or anything of this nature. Cultured fermentation is when you take a uh cooked product like a a bean or anything, and then you add a culture to it. Because of course when you cook it, you kill the bacteria, so you can't cultivate that naturally. So you add a culture to it, and this is called cultured fermentation. These are gigantic wings on the same building of fermentation, but I'm gonna be looking to get into fermented uh cultured fermentation. I excited to try it, just wanted to share that with you all. Hey everybody. Petrochemical products are wonderful because they break down very slowly. They create decay resistant substances that we can then work with. Our linoleum countertops, our paper products that are extra water resilient. Our our clothing, everything that surrounds everything that we're built of, everything that we do is covered in petrochemicals because they provide a layer of resistance to biological breakdown. This is actually the problem with the petrochemicals in our environment, is that they break down at a much much slower rate than organic matter. And as organic matter is churned and revived and recycled, the petrochemical products just kind of break down to smaller and smaller units because they're not chemically breaking down. They're staying in the environment and accumulating. And this is a huge issue because it's guming up our decay cycle. It's gumming up the process of reinvigorating the carbon that the plants have taken out of the atmosphere. And as we do this, we're going to find that this breakdown that's not occurring is causing all sorts of problems because these petrochemicals, as they do degrade, degrade into lots of cancerous things and they start to corrupt our carbon chains and they start to interfere with basic life. And as they break down, they just become smaller and smaller and broadcast more and more finely. We have to realize that we cannot make our disposable products with petrochemicals. We need to take the PFAS out of the paper products. We need to take the all the different petrochemicals that we're using for say plastic bags and stop doing that because it's accumulating our environment at such a rate that we can't the environment can't keep up, and we're already seeing alarming loss of insects, which is the front line of the decay cycle. And who knows what's going on with the bacteria level, but we can assume it's not good. And eventually we're going to find ourselves in a place where the world isn't breaking down according to its natural processes. This could be huge. This could affect all biocarbon-based life. We need to realize that disposable products need to break down easily, and covering everything with petrochemicals is the problem. This is not just a uh cultural problem, this is a civilizational problem. This is a life problem. We endanger the most basic forms of life doing this, and it's becoming worse and worse because there's such a huge mass of these petrochemicals, and they are breaking down finer and finer and finer and broadcasting more and more thinly throughout the environment.
